undefinedUseful Freebiesundefined

undefined

The SSH package includes two more useful programs: Secure Copy (undefinedscp) and Secure FTP (undefinedsftp). As the names suggest, these programs are used to copy and transfer files by FTP via SSH. The basic syntax for the two programs is similar.undefined

undefined

For example, to copy undefinedtest.txt from your home directory on the remote machine to your current working directory, enter:undefined

undefined 
undefinedscp RemoteComputer:test.txt .
undefined
undefined

Depending on your authentication method, you might need to enter your password; however, the colon is mandatory in all cases. It separates the name of the remote machine from the pathname. Also, you need to specify the local path. The easiest case is your current working directory, which is represented by the dot at the end of the line. To copy multiple files, just type a blank-delimited list of the file names:

undefined 
undefinedscp RemoteComputerA:test1.txt RemoteComputerB:test2.txt .
undefined
undefined

If you use the standard login approach, the client will prompt you to enter your password for each file you copy. If you use the public key method discussed previously, you don’t need to type a password. The command

undefined 
undefinedscp RemoteComputerA:test.txt RemoteComputerB:
undefined
undefined

copies the file from remote computer A to remote computer B. To copy a file as undefinedtuppes from undefined/home/tuppes/files to your local directory, type:undefined

undefined 
undefinedscp undefined[email protected]:files/test.txt .
undefinedundefined
undefined

Unlike SSH, you do not specify the undefined-l username option. Copying in the other direction - local to remote - is just as easy:undefined

undefined 
undefinedscp ./test.txt undefined[email protected]:/files/
undefinedundefined
undefined

undefinedscp copies the undefinedtest.txt from your current working directory to undefined/home/tuppes/files on the remote machine. Again, watch out for the closing colon. The undefinedsftp tool uses the same command structure as undefinedscp but has two operating modes: interactive, like the one you might be familar with from FTP, and batch mode. To use undefinedsftp to retrieve the sample file from the remote machine in batch mode, type:undefined

undefined 
undefinedsftp RemoteComputer:test.txt .
undefined
undefined

If you add undefinedremote_test.txt to the end, the program will give that name the local copy of the file. Typing undefinedsftp RemoteComputer opens an interactive, encrypted FTP session on the remote machine, and the server will accept FTP commands such as GET or PUT in the session.undefined

undefined

undefinedBuilding Tunnelsundefined

undefined

SSH also lets you encapsulate other protocols. For example, you can run the Telnet protocol over an encrypted SSH connection and do it transparently for users. The technical term for encapsulating one protocol inside another is “tunneling.”

undefined

The standard specifies that programs must be running on the same machine to use the tunnel. If you want other machines on the network to use the tunnel, you must specify undefined-o GatewayPorts=yes when setting up the tunnel. The alternative is to set the option in undefinedssh_config.undefined

undefined

This setup is similar to a Virtual Private Network (VPN) connection but is easier to implement. The SSH variant has the disadvantage that you can only forward a single TCP port. Thus, you need an SSH tunnel for each port you want to forward. If you want to encrypt all communication between two machines, a VPN is probably a better choice.

undefined

Any user can set up a tunnel, although tunnels for privileged ports (i.e., below 1024) are reserved for root. To open a tunnel to a remote machine encapsulating the Telnet protocol (port 23), enter:

undefined 
undefinedssh -c blowfish -L 23 RemoteBox:23RemoteBox
undefined
undefined

The command uses the undefined-L option to open a tunnel from local port 23 on the local machine (the first 23) to port 23 on the remote machine. The fast Blowfish method is used for encryption. If you type two remote machine names , you can take advantage of another SSH feature: opening a tunnel from the first machine, via the second, to a third. The commandundefined

undefined 
undefinedssh -L 23:192.168.1.1:23 192.168.20.5
undefined
undefined

starts the tunnel on the local machine and routes it via an intermediate station (192.168.1.1) to its endpoint. The generic syntax for opening a tunnel from the local machine to the remote computer is thus:

undefined 
undefinedssh -L LocalPort:RemoteComputerA:RemotePort RemoteComputerB
undefined
undefined

For a direct tunnel, the two host designations are identical.

undefined

undefinedTunnel Tricksundefined

undefined

Replacements for the undefinednetstat commands are undefinedss (socket statistics), which takes many of the same options as undefinednetstat, and undefinedip, which replaces undefinedifconfig (see the Networking Tools article). In Figure 4, the undefinednetstat and undefinedss commands demonstrate that a Telnet connection has been set up via SSH. undefined

If you were to look more closely at the syntax used to open a tunnel, you might be led to assume that the local and remote ports do not need to be identical - and this is true. Assuming the remote machine is running a proxy configured for transparent proxying on port 3128, you could redirect all HTTP requests as follows:

undefined 
undefinedssh -o GatewayPorts=yes -L 80:RemoteComputer:3128RemoteComputer
undefined
undefined

This process of redirecting one port to another is called <b>undefinedport forwarding</b>. Other computers on the network also need undefined-o GatewayPorts=yes to use the tunnel.undefined

undefined

Similarly, tunneling works in the reverse direction. The following syntax

undefined 
undefinedssh -o GatewayPorts=yes -R 3128:LocalComputer:80RemoteComputer
undefined
undefined

lets you set up a return tunnel from the remote machine to your computer.

undefined

undefinedGraphical Tunnelsundefined

undefined

The X Window System is natively network-capable, but almost nobody uses this ability because communications are again unencrypted over the wire. Tunneling with SSH makes this a far more attractive proposal.

undefined

To tunnel X11, the SSH daemon (undefinedsshd) emulates an X server and occupies a display (number 11 by default). When you log in to the server, the server sets the undefinedDISPLAY environment variable to this value, or to undefinedlocalhost:11.0 to be more precise. The idea is to avoid collisions with the X server running locally. Information sent by a computer to this display is encrypted and sent to your machine.undefined

undefined

OpenSUSE 11.0 enables X11 forwarding (the technical term for the process) by default. You can disable X11 forwarding on the machine configured for forwarding by setting the undefinedX11Forwarding variable to undefinedno in undefinedetc/ssh/sshd_config. The undefinedX11DisplayOffset variable with a default value of undefined10 defines the distance between the virtual display and the physical display; you should keep the default here.undefined

undefined

If the machine on which you want to display tunneled X11 is an OpenSUSE 11.0 machine, the undefinedetc/ssh/ssh_config file will already have the undefinedForwardX11Trusted variable set to undefinedyes.undefined

undefined

Next, log in to the remote machine and launch, for example, the undefinedXclock program. Figure 5 (Linux Shell Handbook - 2021 Edition, pg. 75) shows the display (undefinedlocalhost:11.0), the process, and the matching network connections.undefined

undefinedOther Practical Usesundefined

undefined

SSH can be put to othe clever uses to. For example, the popular backup tool undefinedrsync is perfectly capable of copying files via SSH. Thus, you can easily implement an offsite backup solution via undefinedrsync and SSH, for which you need to install OpenSSH and rsync on both the source and destination machines. To perform an offsite backup, specify the path on the remote host as the destination:undefined

undefined 
undefinedrsync -avzh /path/to/local/dir undefined[email protected]:/path/to/remote/dir
undefinedundefined
undefined

Usually, you don’t have to craft any special command to use rsync via SSH. However, if you are using a non-standard port or a non-default private key location, you’ll need to use the undefined-e switch:undefined

undefined 
undefinedrsync -avzhe 'ssh -p 1777' /path/to/local/dir undefined[email protected]:/path/to/remote/dir
undefinedundefined
undefined

As described earlier, you can use undefinedscp to copy files securely via SSH. For more efficient compression and transfer, however, you might want to use a simple trick that allows you to copy multiple files and directories between local and remote machines by piping a undefinedtar datastream to SSH. Simply put, you can use the undefinedtar tool to compress several files or a directory and send the resulting archive to a remote host via SSH:undefined

undefined 
undefinedtar zcf - DIR | ssh undefined[email protected] 'tar zxf -'
undefinedundefined
undefined

This command compresses the contents on the DIR directory, passes the resulting archive to SSH, and then decompresses the archive on the remote host. Of course, you can perform the same action in the opposite direction, from the remote host to the local machine:

undefined

undefinedSynchronizing Data with Rsyncundefined

undefined

Rsync is the perfect synchronization tool for keeping your data in sync. The program manages file properties and uses SSH to encrypt your data, and it is perfect for transferring large volumes of data, if the target computer has a copy of a previous version.

undefined

Rsync checks for differences between the source and target versions. The tool that has been developed by the Samba team uses an efficient checksum-search algorithm for comparing data; rsync only transfers the differences between the two sides and therefore saves time and bandwidth.

undefined

undefinedIn Syncundefined

undefined

The generic syntax for rsync is undefinedrsync [options] source target, where undefinedtarget can be a local target on the same machine or a remote target on another machine. The choice of source and target is critical; decide carefully in which direction you will be synchronizing to avoid loss of data. If you’re not sure that you are using the correct options or the correct source/target, you can run rsync with the undefined-n flag to tell the program to perform a trial run. Additionally, you can increase the amount of information by defining undefined-v and switching to verbose output.undefined

undefined

To mirror a directory undefineddir1 on a local machine, for example, type:undefined

undefined 
undefined$ rsync dir1/* dir2/
skipping directory foo
skipping directory bar
skipping non-regular file "text.txt"
undefined
undefined

As the output shows, rsync would transfer normal files but leave out subdirectories and symbolic links (undefinednon-regular file). To transfer directories recursively down to the lowest level, you should specify the undefined-r option. Using the undefined-l flag additionally picks up your symlinks. Of course, a combination of the options is also possible:undefined

undefined 
undefinedrsync -lr dir1/* dir2/
undefined
undefined

Rsync has an alternative approach to handling symlinks. If you replace undefined-l with undefined-L, the program will resolve the link, and your former symlinks will end up as “normal” files at the target.undefined

undefined

Be careful with the slash - appending a slash to a directory name influences the way rsync handles an operation (see the “Common Rsync Traps” box).

undefined

undefinedAs You Wereundefined

undefined

If you will be using rsync to create backups, it makes sense to keep the attributes of the original files. By attributes I mean permissions and timestamps - that is, information on the last access time (undefinedatime), the last status change (undefinedctime), and the last modification (undefinedmtime) .undefined

undefined

Additionally, administrators can benefit from parameters that preserve owner and group data and support device files. To retain the permissions, just specify the undefined-p option; undefined-t handles the timestamps, and undefined-g keeps the group membership.undefined

undefined

Whereas any normal user can specify these parameters, the undefined-o (keep the owner data) and undefined-D (device attributes) flags are available only to root. The complete command line with all these options could look like this:undefined

undefined 
undefinedrsync -rlptgoD /home/huhn /backup
undefined
undefined

Don’t worry - you don’t have to remember all these options. Rsync offers a practical shortcut and a special option that combines these parameters for this case. Instead of undefined-rlptgoD, just type undefined-a.undefined

undefined

undefinedExclusiveundefined

undefined

Rsync has another practical option that allows you to exclude certain files from the synchronization process. To leverage this feature, specify the undefined--exclude= option and a search pattern and define the files to exclude. With this option, you can use wildcards:undefined

undefined 
undefinedrsync -a --exclude=*.wav ~/music backup/
undefined
undefined

This example excludes large WAV files that end in undefined.wav from the backup of a music collection. If you need to exclude MP3s as well, just append another undefinedexclude statement and a pattern:undefined

undefined 
undefinedrsync -a --exclude=*.wav --exclude=*.mp3 ...
undefined
undefined

To save time, you can store your exclusions in a text file. To do this, you will need a separate line for each search pattern. Specify the undefined--exclude-from=file_with_exclusions parameter to parse the file.undefined

undefined

undefinedTidying Upundefined

undefined

Rsync offers various parameters for deleting data that is no longer needed or wanted. To get rid of files in your backup that no longer exist in the source, type undefined--delete. Rsync’s default behavior is to delete files before the transfer is finished. Alternatively, you can define undefineddelete-after to delete files of the target after all the syncing is done.undefined

undefined

Additionally, you can tell rsync to delete files that you have excluded. For example, imagine you’ve decided that you no longer want the MP3s in the backup and you’ve started to exclude them with undefined--exclude=*.mp3. Now you can define undefined--delete-excluded, and rsync will recognize that those files are no longer wanted.undefined

undefined

All undefined--delete options have basically the same goal: to keep an exact copy of the original. If you don’t use the switch, you will have to clean up manually; otherwise, the files that you’ve decided are useless will remain. Use these options with care.undefined

undefined

undefinedCommon Rsync Trapsundefined

undefined

Some rsync options could cause trouble if you don’t use them with caution. Being aware of these common mistakes can help.

undefined
    undefined
  • Most users find the final slash for directories confusing at first. For example, if you call undefinedrsync -a source/folder target/, rsync will transfer the directory called undefinedfolder and its contents to the undefinedtarget directory. If the directory undefinedfolder doesn’t exist, rsync will create it. If you append a slash toundefinedsource/folder, rsync will only transfer the contents of undefinedfolder. That means a file undefinedsource/folder/foo.txt is being transferred to undefinedtarget/foo.txt instead of undefinedtarget/folder/foo.txt.undefined
    • undefined
  • An absolute classic troublemaker is the option undefined--delete. If you get source and target mixed up, undefined--delete will happily delete several original files. To be on the safe side, remember to use undefined-n in a test run.undefined
    • undefined
  • If a transfer is interrupted and you’re using the undefined--partial flag, rsync saves parts of the file under the same name as the original, which is not always helpful. Imagine that you’re using rsync to update a large and existing ISO image of your favorite distribution (like a Release Candidate). The transfer of the new version gets interrupted after just a few bytes. Rsync will overwrite your original file with the smaller part of the ISO image from the server, and you will have lost your current file and have to start from scratch. To avoid data loss in this scenario, you can create a hard link before calling rsync. If the transfer fails now, you won’t lose the ISO image; instead the partial file will be given a new name without destroying the original.undefined
    • undefined
undefined

undefinedTuning Rsyncundefined

undefined

Several options increase rsync’s performance. Often, I use the undefined-z switch to compress data when I sync data over a network connection. Figure 1 shows this using Grsync, the graphical front end to rsync. If the connection is very slow, you can also define a bandwidth limit. To transfer data with only 20KBps, for example, use:undefined

undefined 
undefinedrsync ... --bwlimit=20
undefined
undefined

Rsync is perfect for transferring large volumes of data. If you specify the undefined--partial parameter and the transfer is interrupted for some reason, you can pick up the transfer from the point at which you left off. Specifying the undefined--progress option gives you a progress indicator to let you keep track of the transfer operation:undefined

undefined 
undefined$ rsync -avz --progress --partial remote.server:/home/huhn/music/folk ~/music/
receiving file list ...
42 files to consider
...
12_Moladh_Uibhist.mp3
   1143849   4%  339.84kB/s   0:01:10
undefined
undefined

At the other end of the connection, the partial file is hidden in the target directory at first. Typing undefinedls -a reveals a file called undefined12_Moladh_Uibhist.mp3.7rUSSq. The dot at the start of the file name keeps the file hidden, and the arbitrary extension removes the danger of overwriting existing files.undefined

undefined

When the transfer completes, the file gets its original name back. If the transfer is interrupted, you can restart by specifying the undefined--partial option again. Alternatively, you have a shortcut: If you want to use a combination of undefined--partial and undefined--progress, simply use undefined-P.undefined

undefined

Rsync keeps your data up to date and you stay on top of confusing version changes. Its options help you manage file properties, and it works well with SSH. When you need to transfer large volumes of data, rsync comes to your rescue.

undefinedEscape Sequencesundefined

undefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefined
CharactersMeaning
\Escape following character

\EEnd literal sequence
undefined

“Escaping” is a way of treating characters which have a special meaning in regular expressions literally, rather than as special characters.

undefined

undefinedCommon Metacharactersundefined

undefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefinedundefined
CharactersMeaning
^ (Caret)means “the beginning of the line”. So “^a” means find a line starting with an “a”.
[] (Square Brackets)specifies a range. If you did m[a,o,u]m it can become: mam, mum, mom if you did: m[a-d]m it can become anything that starts and ends with m and has any character a to d inbetween. For example, these would work: mam, mbm, mcm, mdm. This kind of wildcard specifies an “or” relationship (you only need one to match).
[!]This construct is similar to the [  ] construct, except rather than matching any characters inside the brackets, it’ll match any character, as long as it is not listed between the [ and ]. This is a logical NOT. For example rm myfile[!9] will remove all myfiles* (ie. myfiles1, myfiles2 etc) but won’t remove a file with the number 9 anywhere within it’s name.
[^]This is the equivalent of [!] in standard wildcards. This performs a logical “not”. This will match anything that is not listed within those square brackets. For example, undefinedrm myfile[^9] will remove all myfiles* (ie. myfiles1, myfiles2 etc) but won’t remove a file with the number 9 anywhere within it’s name.undefined
{} (Curly braces)terms are separated by commas and each term must be the name of something, or a wildcard. This wildcard will copy anything that matches either wildcard(s), or exact name(s) (an “or” relationship, one or the other). For example undefinedcp {*.doc,*.pdf} ~ will copy anything ending with .doc or .pdf to the users home directory. Note that spaces are not allowed after the commas (or anywhere else).undefined
() ParenthesesUsed for capturing groups.
\The escape character is usually \ , i.e. to protect a subsequent special character. Thus, “\\” searches for a backslash. Note you may need to use quotation marks and backslash(es).
.will match any single character, equivalent to ? (question mark) in standard wildcard expressions. Thus, “m.a” matches “mpa” and “mea” but not “ma” or “mppa”.
.*is used to match any string, equivalent to * in standard wildcards.
*this item is to be matched zero or more times. ie. n* will match n, nn, nnnn, nnnnnnn but not na or any other character.
$means “the end of the line”. So “a$” means find a line ending with an “a”. For example, this command searches the file myfile for lines starting with an “s” and ending with an “n”. undefinedcat myfile | grep '^s.*n$'undefined
|This wildcard makes a logical OR relationship between wildcards. This way you can search for something or something else (possibly using two different regular expressions). You may need to add a ‘\’ (backslash) before this command to work, because the shell may attempt to interpret this as a pipe.
?Match 0 or 1 of the preceding token
+Match 1 or more of the preceding token
<Matches a “<” character
>Matches a “>” character
undefined

undefinedHardware Helpundefined

undefined

In modern Linux, you can work with hardware in several different ways: general information commands, the dmidecode command, viewing the undefined/proc and undefined/sys virtual filesystems, manipulating kernel modules, modifying daemon configuration files, and working with systemd.undefined

undefined

For the sake of completeness, undefinedhdparm (hardware parameters) should also be mentioned. hdparm works with the Linux SATA/PATA/SAS libATA subsystem, the older IDE, and some USB drives released after 2008. It does not work with most solid state drives, and functional options may vary with the kernel. If hdparm does work on your system, it allows extensive customization, including setting power management features, 32-bit I/O support on IDE drives, and onboard defect management features. However, the wrong options can crash a system and brick a hard drive, and some options have warnings in the man page. If you can use hdparm, it is a powerful tool, but unless you understand exactly what an option does, you are better off avoiding the command altogether.undefined

undefinedWorking with Traditional Configuration Files (source: LINUX SHELL HANDBOOK - 2021 EDITION, page 28)undefined

undefined

Although systemd is today the norm for launching services at boot time, there are still services traditionally called “daemons,” that are shell scripts. Distributions use shell-like configuration files to configure these services easily, without having to invent a new syntax for each configuration. You can usually recognize these configuration files because they have:

undefined
    undefined
  • undefined

    Shell variables (written in capital letters and with no spaces), like undefinedBLUETOOTH_ENABLED = 1 in undefined/etc/default/bluetoothundefined

    undefined
    • undefined
  • undefined

    Comments explaining what each variable means

    undefined
    • undefined
  • undefined

    Shell functions (sometimes) to extend or override features in existing scripts

    undefined
    • undefined
undefined

In Debian-based systems, most of these config scripts are placed in <i>/etc/default/*</i> and can be edited in a text editor as root. If you make changes to any system shell script or write your own system scripts, please keep the following facts in mind:

undefined
    undefined
  • If these scripts run as root, they have the potential to destroy your systemif you add the wrong command or accidentally activate a command that is supposed to be commented out. To prevent accidents, always backup a configuration file before editing it.
    • undefined
  • Scripts are usually called with an “include” to another script by the dot (.) shell command, and the calling script will terminate if an undefinedexit appears.undefined
    • undefined
  • In the shell, no spaces are allowed before and after the equal sign (=) when setting variables. KDE and Gnome config files frequently have spaces everywhere to make them easier to read, but in the shell, a space means separation of a command and its parameters, which can cause a syntax error. Values or options containing spaces should have quotes around them.
    • undefined
undefined

undefinedUsing systemd (source: LINUX SHELL HANDBOOK - 2021 EDITION, page 29)undefined

undefined

Systemd began as a replacement for undefinedinit for starting daemons and soon morphed into a general system manager. Configuration files are stored within undefined/etc/systemd. General information is stored in nine basic files in undefined/etc/systemd, most of which has self-explanatory names, including ones for login, the logfile, and the network. Systemd configuration files typically begin with a series of fields in a section labeled undefined[Manager] that can be edited freely. The most important of these files is undefinedsystem.conf. All the files are detailed in undefinedsystemd-user.conf(5) in the man pages.undefined

undefined

For editing individual system resources, systemd uses the undefinedsystemctl utility. undefinedsystemctl works with units, or system resources configuration files, usually as a three-part structure:undefined

undefined 
undefinedsystemctl [sub-command] [unit]
undefined
undefined

For example, the major sub-commands for system services are undefinedstatus, undefinedenable, or undefineddisable.undefined

undefined

Each unit has its own configuration file, rather than being edited like traditional configuration files, unit configuration files can be provided an override file or a full-replacement fileby using the undefinedsystemctl command. Overrides are stored in undefined/etc/systemd/system. To be used, a new override must be activated with undefinedsystemctl daemon-reload. Running the command undefinedsystemd-delta shows all the overrides currently running on the system. An override marked undefined[EXTENDED] shows the location of the override file, whereas one marked undefined[OVERRIDDEN] shows the difference between the original and the currently used replacement unit file.undefined

undefined

Although controversial when first introduced, systemd is a far more orderly approach than the other methods of configuring or finding information. However it does introduce numerous new concepts that can be overwhelming at first. As with any other methods, be sure you know what you are doing with systemd before you actually edit one of its files. See the article on systemd elsewhere in this issue.

undefined

These sources of information and ways to edit are a lot to absorb. Unsurprisingly, functions are often duplicated between different sources. If you change the configuration, usually you should use the same approach consistently, so you can keep track of all the changes made more easily. In many modern systems, the most straightforward approach is to use systemd. However, experienced users often prefer to edit configuration files, a practice that is as old as Linux itself - if not older. Most of the time, what matters is not the method so much as consistency.

undefined

undefinedSystemd Primerundefined

undefined

Systemd has gradually replaced the ancient System V as the leading init system for Linux. Most mainstream Linux varients now use systemd. The init system launches processes when you start your Linux system, and it also stays around to start and stop services while the system is running. Systemd also logs system events, automates processes, and much more.

undefined

undefinedAnatomy of a Unit Fileundefined

undefined

In systemd parlance, a managed object is known as a undefinedunit. The files that are used to initialize and start units at boot time are known as undefinedunit files. Admins will find the unit files in folders such as:undefined

undefined
    undefined
  • <i>/etc/systemd/system/*</i>
    • undefined
  • <i>/run/systemd/system/*</i>
    • undefined
  • <i>/usr/lib/systemd/system/*</i>
    • undefined
undefined

Unit files serve a role that is similar to the init scripts of older Linux systems; however, a unit file is not executable. Instead, a unit file is more like a configuration file in the style of Windows undefined.ini files.undefined

undefined

The undefined[Unit] section contains a human-readable description of the service; the undefinedAfter variable specifies other services that need to start first. You could use the undefinedBefore variable to declare that the service you are defining with the unit file must start before the service(s) specified with the variable.undefined

undefined

The [undefinedService] section sets the user account and group that the database server. undefinedType determines the boot style. undefinedSimple means that the program specified below undefinedExecStart starts the main process. The two MySQL scripts specified below undefinedExecStartPre handle the preparatory work.undefined

undefined

undefinedExecStartPost calls scripts that need to run after the main program starts. The undefinedmysql-wait-ready script makes sure MySQL completes the cleanup that it normally performs at start-up time. This means that services that require MySQL do not start until the database is actually ready to accept connections.undefined

undefined

Additionally, the unit file sets a timeout and assigns the database service to the multiuser target. This target is a special unit that basically assumes the role of the previous runlevel 3 in System V, which starts the system normally in multiuser mode.

undefined

undefinedMore Securityundefined

undefined

Unit files support a slew of other parameters, including options that provide an easy way for improving the security of your services.

undefined

The first of these parameters in the [undefinedService] section is:undefined

undefined 
undefinedPrivateNetwork=yes
undefined
undefined

This setting completely isolates the service from any networks. The service then only sees a loopback device, and even that does not have a connection to the host’s actual loopback device. Of course, this option is not very useful for network-based services.

undefined

A word of caution: Sometimes you need a network, even if the need is not apparent at first glance. For instance, a service might perform most of its work locally but use Lightweight Directory Access Protocol (LDAP) to handle authentication. In that case, you need to be sure only users with a user ID below 1000 are authenticated; names need to resolve to UIDs locally through undefined/etc/passwd for these accounts.undefined

undefined

A second security feature in [Service] is:

undefined 
undefinedPrivateTmp=yes
undefined
undefined

If this option is set, the service uses its own undefined/tmp directory instead of the global undefined/tmp, which protects the service against malicious Symlink and DoS attacks that tend to use undefined/tmp. However, keep in mind that some services locate communication sockets in undefined/tmp that will not work if they are in a private directory.undefined

undefined

The next two options let you prevent services from writing to specific directories or even accessing them in any way:

undefined 
undefinedReadOnlyDirectories=/var
InaccessibleDirectories=/home
undefined
undefined

Linux provides a means for assigning the privileges traditionally associated with superuser. These privileges are known as undefinedcapabilities, and you can see the list of all available capabilities by viewing the undefinedcapabilities man page:undefined

undefined 
undefinedman capabilities
undefined
undefined

Systemd additionally lets you assign specific capabilities to a service or withdraw those capabilities through settings in the unit file. For example, the following line in the [Service] section of the unit file:

undefined 
undefinedCapabilityBoundingSet=CAP_CHOWN CAP_KILL
undefined
undefined

defines a whitelist of capabilities that the process must have.

undefined

Defining such a whitelist is not always easy. The other option is to take capabilities away from the service. If you prepend the capability with a tilde (~), this capability is explicitly taken away.

undefined

You can also use the unit file to limit the resources a service can access. The undefinedsetrlimit() man page lists all restrictable resources. For example, if you set the maximum size of a file (FSIZE) that the service is allowed to generate to 0, as shown in the example below, the service cannot write the file anywhere. If you specify 1 as the maximum number of processes (NPROC) the service is allowed to spawn, the service cannot fork any other processes.undefined

undefined 
undefinedLimitNPROC=1
LimitFSIZE=0
undefined
undefined

You can limit other resources in a similar way.

undefined

undefinedMonitoring Processesundefined

undefined

After your system boots, you might want to know whether all the required services are actually running. The undefinedsystemctl command provides an overview of service status. undefinedSystemctl lists all booted services with status information. If you only want to see the failed startups, try:undefined

undefined 
undefinedsystemctl --state=failed
undefined
undefined

For a single service, you can view more detailed information with:

undefined 
undefinedsystemctl status mysql.service
undefined
undefined

The output shows the exit states of the pre- and post-scripts from the unit file, as well as additional information on the service status.

undefined

The status messages can be quite long on a case-by-case basis. Admins can thus use either the undefinedn line number parameter to limit the number of rows to output or the undefinedo file parameter to redirect everything to a file.undefined

undefined

undefinedStarting and Stoppingundefined

undefined

Sometimes you need to stop or restart individual services after a boot or reboot. undefinedSystemctl and the undefinedstop, undefinedstart, undefinedrestart, and undefinedreload commands can help; for example:undefined

undefined 
undefinedsystemctl stop mysqld
systemctl start mysqld
undefined
undefined

A user who wants to start or stop a system service must authenticate. If the process does not respond to the undefinedstop command, the only way out is:undefined

undefined 
undefinedsystemctl kill unit_name
undefined
undefined

This command sends a kill signal to each process in the process group, even to those that the parent process forked at a later stage. The effect thus resembles undefinedkillall process name. The undefined-s option also lets you send another specific signal to a process, for example, undefinedSIGHUP to trigger a reload, as shown in the following example:undefined

undefined 
undefinedsystemctl kill -s HUP --kill-who=main crond.service
undefined
undefined

The --kill -who option ensures that only the undefinedmain process receives the signal.undefined
